The court system consists of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Ireland" title="Supreme Court of Ireland">Supreme Court</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Appeal_(Ireland)" title="Court of Appeal (Ireland)">Court of Appeal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_(Ireland)" title="High Court (Ireland)">High Court</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_Court_(Ireland)" title="Circuit Court (Ireland)">Circuit Court</a> and the <a href="/wiki/District_Court_(Ireland)" title="District Court (Ireland)">District Court</a>, all of which apply the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Law of the Republic of Ireland">Irish law</a> and hear both civil and criminal matters. Trials for serious offences must usually be held before a <a href="/wiki/Jury" title="Jury">jury</a>. The High Court, Court of Appeal and the Supreme Court have authority, by means of <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Judicial review in the Republic of Ireland">judicial review</a>, to determine the compatibility of laws and activities of other institutions of the state with the constitution and the law. Except in exceptional circumstances, court hearings must occur in public.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg/220px-CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg/330px-CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg/440px-CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CriminalCourtofJusticeDublin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Courts_of_Justice,_Dublin" title="Criminal Courts of Justice, Dublin">Criminal Courts of Justice</a> is the principal building for criminal courts</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na">Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na na h\xc3\x89ireann</a> (<i>Guardians of the Peace of Ireland</i>), more commonly referred to as the Garda\xc3\xad, is the state\'s civilian police force. The force is responsible for all aspects of civil policing, both in terms of territory and infrastructure. It is headed by the Garda Commissioner, who is appointed by the Government. Most uniformed members do not routinely carry <a href="/wiki/Firearms_legislation_in_Ireland" title="Firearms legislation in Ireland">firearms</a>. Standard policing is traditionally carried out by uniformed officers equipped only with a <a href="/wiki/Baton_(law_enforcement)" title="Baton (law enforcement)">baton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pepper_spray" title="Pepper spray">pepper spray</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Military_Police_Corps_(Ireland)" title="Military Police Corps (Ireland)">Military Police</a> is the corps of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Army" title="Irish Army">Irish Army</a> responsible for the provision of policing service personnel and providing a military police presence to forces while on exercise and deployment. In wartime, additional tasks include the provision of a traffic control organisation to allow rapid movement of military formations to their mission areas. Other wartime roles include control of <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> and refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland\'s <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationality_law" title="Irish nationality law">citizenship laws</a> relate to "the island of Ireland", including islands and seas, thereby extending them to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, which is part of the United Kingdom. Therefore, anyone born in Northern Ireland who meets the requirements for being an Irish citizen, such as birth on the island of Ireland to an Irish or British citizen parent or a parent who is entitled to live in Northern Ireland or the Republic without restriction on their residency,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> may exercise an entitlement to Irish citizenship, such as an <a href="/wiki/Irish_passport" title="Irish passport">Irish passport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Foreign relations of the Republic of Ireland">Foreign relations of the Republic of Ireland</a></div><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ireland%E2%80%93NATO_relations" title="Ireland\xe2\x80\x93NATO relations">Ireland\xe2\x80\x93NATO relations</a></div>\n<p>Foreign relations are substantially influenced by membership of the European Union, although bilateral relations with the United Kingdom and United States are also important.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> It held the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_the_Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Presidency of the Council of the European Union">Presidency of the Council of the European Union</a> on six occasions, most recently from January to June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg/220px-Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg/330px-Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg/440px-Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2942" data-file-height="1959" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Institutions_europeennes_IMG_4300.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ireland has been a <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">member state of the European Union</a> since 1973.</div></div></div>\n<p>Ireland tends towards independence in foreign policy; thus the country is not a member of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and has a <a href="/wiki/The_Emergency_(Ireland)#Neutrality" title="The Emergency (Ireland)">longstanding</a> policy of military neutrality. This policy has helped the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Defence_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Defence Forces">Irish Defence Forces</a> to be successful in their contributions to peace-keeping missions with the United Nations since 1960, during the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a> and subsequently in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement"><span title="This claim has reliable sources with contradicting facts (November 2015)">disputed</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Republic_of_Ireland#Talk_page_section" title="Talk:Republic of Ireland">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Irish_neutrality_during_World_War_II" title="Irish neutrality during World War II">Irish neutrality during World War II</a>, Ireland had more than 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Participants_in_World_War_II#Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Participants in World War II">participants in the war</a> through enlistment in the British armed forces. During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Irish military policy, while ostensibly neutral, was biased towards NATO.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_Lemass" title="Se\xc3\xa1n Lemass">Se\xc3\xa1n Lemass</a> authorised the search of Cuban and Czechoslovak aircraft passing through Shannon and passed the information to the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Ireland\'s air facilities were used by the United States military for the delivery of military personnel involved in the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a> through <a href="/wiki/Shannon_Airport" title="Shannon Airport">Shannon Airport</a>. The airport had previously been used for the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">invasion of Afghanistan</a> in 2001, as well as the <a href="/wiki/First_Gulf_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Gulf War">First Gulf War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 1999, Ireland has been a member of NATO\'s <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">Partnership for Peace</a> (PfP) program and NATO\'s <a href="/wiki/Euro-Atlantic_Partnership_Council" title="Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council">Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council</a> (EAPC), which is aimed at creating trust between NATO and other states in Europe and the former Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Defence_Forces_(Ireland)" title="Defence Forces (Ireland)">Defence Forces (Ireland)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_(14173266347).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg/260px-State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg" width="260" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg/390px-State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg/520px-State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_%2814173266347%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5724" data-file-height="2436" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Visit_by_The_President_of_the_Republic_of_Mozambique011_(14173266347).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soldiers of the <i>Garda On\xc3\xb3r</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The Defence Forces are made up of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Army" title="Irish Army">Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Naval_Service" title="Irish Naval Service">Naval Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Air_Corps" title="Irish Air Corps">Air Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Defence_Forces" title="Reserve Defence Forces">Reserve Defence Force</a>. It is small but well equipped, with almost 10,000 full-time military personnel and over 2,000 in reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Ireland is a <a href="/wiki/Neutral_country" title="Neutral country">neutral country</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and has "triple-lock" rules governing the participation of Irish troops in conflict zones, whereby approval must be given by the UN, the <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il" class="mw-redirect" title="D\xc3\xa1il">D\xc3\xa1il</a> and Government.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Daily deployments of the Defence Forces cover <a href="/wiki/Military_aid_to_the_civil_power" title="Military aid to the civil power">aid to civil power</a> operations, protection and patrol of Irish territorial waters and <a href="/wiki/EEZ" class="mw-redirect" title="EEZ">EEZ</a> by the Irish Naval Service, and UN, EU and <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">PfP</a> peace-keeping missions. By 1996, over 40,000 Irish service personnel had served in international UN peacekeeping missions.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish Air Corps is the air component of the Defence Forces and operates sixteen fixed wing aircraft and eight helicopters. The Irish Naval Service is Ireland\'s navy, and operates eight <a href="/wiki/Patrol_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrol ship">patrol ships</a>, and smaller numbers of inflatable boats and training vessels, and has armed boarding parties capable of seizing a ship and a special unit of <a href="/wiki/Frogman" title="Frogman">frogmen</a>. The military includes the Reserve Defence Forces (<a href="/wiki/Army_Reserve_(Ireland)" title="Army Reserve (Ireland)">Army Reserve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_Service_Reserve" title="Naval Service Reserve">Naval Service Reserve</a>) for part-time reservists. Ireland\'s special forces include the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ranger_Wing" title="Army Ranger Wing">Army Ranger Wing</a>, which trains and operates with international special operations units. The President is the formal Supreme Commander of the Defence Forces, but in practice these Forces answer to the Government via the <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Defence_(Ireland)" title="Minister for Defence (Ireland)">Minister for Defence</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Economy of the Republic of Ireland">Economy of the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueEurozone.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/220px-BlueEurozone.svg.png" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/330px-BlueEurozone.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/440px-BlueEurozone.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="645" data-file-height="690" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueEurozone.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ireland is part of the EU (dark blue &amp; light blue) and <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> (dark blue).</div></div></div>\n<p>Ireland is an open economy (6th on the <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a>), and ranks first for "high-value" FDI flows.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>  Using the metric global <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP per capita</a>, Ireland ranks 5th of 187 (IMF) and 6th of 175 (World Bank). The alternative metric <a href="/wiki/Modified_gross_national_income" title="Modified gross national income">modified Gross National Income (GNI)</a> is intended to give a more accurate view of "activity in the domestic economy".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  This is particularly relevant in Ireland \'s small globalized economy as GDP includes income from non-Irish owned companies which flows out of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>  Indeed, foreign multinationals are the driver of Ireland\'s economy, employing a quarter of the private sector workforce,<sup id="cite_ref-oecdx_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecdx-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and paying 80% of Irish business taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-RTE_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RTE-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ctx1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ctx1-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>  14 of Ireland\'s top 20 firms (by 2017 turnover) are US-based multinationals<sup id="cite_ref-itimes_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itimes-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> (80% of foreign mutinationals in Ireland are US;<sup id="cite_ref-idax_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idax-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iday_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iday-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> there are no non-US/no-UK foreign firms in Ireland\'s top 50 firms by turnover, and only one by employees being German retailer Lidl at No. 41<sup id="cite_ref-itimes_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itimes-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>).\n</p><p>Ireland adopted the euro currency in 2002 along with eleven other <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Member states of the European Union">EU member states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The country officially exited recession in 2010, assisted by a growth in exports from US multinationals in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> However, due to a rise in the cost of public borrowing due to government guarantees of private banking debt, the Irish government accepted an \xe2\x82\xac85 billion programme of assistance from the EU, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) and bilateral loans from the United Kingdom, Sweden and Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Following three years of contraction, the economy grew by 0.7% in 2011 and 0.9% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The unemployment rate was 14.7% in 2012, including 18.5% among recent immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In March 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment rate">unemployment rate</a> was reported by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Statistics_Office_(Ireland)" title="Central Statistics Office (Ireland)">CSO</a> to be 8.6%, down from a peak unemployment rate of 15.1% in February 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In addition to unemployment, net emigration from Ireland between 2008 and 2013 totalled 120,100,<sup id="cite_ref-Financial_Times_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Times-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> or some 2.6% of the total population according to the <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Ireland_2011" title="Census of Ireland 2011">Census of Ireland 2011</a>. One-third of the emigrants were aged between 15 and 24.<sup id="cite_ref-Financial_Times_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Times-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland exited its EU-IMF bailout programme on 15 December 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Having implemented budget cuts, reforms and sold assets, Ireland was again able to access debt markets. Since then, Ireland has been able to sell long term bonds at record rates.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  However, the stabilisation of the Irish credit bubble required a large transfer of debt from the private sector balance sheet (highest OECD leverage), to the public sector balance sheet (almost unleveraged, pre-crisis), via Irish bank bailouts and public deficit spending.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>  The transfer of this debt means that Ireland, in 2017, still has one of the highest levels of both public sector indebtedness, and private sector indebtedness, in the EU-28/OECD.<sup id="cite_ref-grek_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grek-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grek2_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grek2-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-debt0_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debt0-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-debt1_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debt1-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland continues to de-leverage its domestic private sector while growing its US multinational-driven economy.  Ireland became the main destination for US <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland#Corporate_tax_inversions" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">corporate tax inversions</a> from 2009-2016 (mostly pharmaceutical), peaking with the blocked $160bn Allergan/Pfizer inversion (worlds largest inversion, and circa 85% of Irish <a href="/wiki/Modified_gross_national_income" title="Modified gross national income">GNI*</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-inver_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inver-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pfizer2_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfizer2-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>  Ireland also became the largest foreign location for US "big cap" technology multinationals (i.e. Apple, Google, Microsoft, Facebook), which deliverd a GDP growth rate of 26.3% (and GNP growth rate of 18.7%) in 2015.  This growth was subsequently shown to be due to Apple restructuring its "<a href="/wiki/Double_Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Double Irish">double Irish</a>" subsidiary (Apple Sales International, currently under threat of a <a href="/wiki/EU_illegal_State_aid_case_against_Apple_in_Ireland" title="EU illegal State aid case against Apple in Ireland">\xe2\x82\xac13bn EU "illegal state aid" fine</a> for preferential tax treatment).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_policy">Taxation policy</span></h3>\n<p>Ireland\'s economy was transformed with the creation of a 10% low-tax "<a href="/wiki/Special_economic_zone" title="Special economic zone">special economic zone</a>", called the <a href="/wiki/International_Financial_Services_Centre" title="International Financial Services Centre">International Financial Services Centre</a> (or "IFSC"), in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-his0_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-his0-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>  In 1999, the entire country was effectively "turned into an IFSC", with the reduction of Irish corporation tax from 32% to 12.5% (the birth of Ireland\'s "low-tax" model).<sup id="cite_ref-his1_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-his1-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-his2_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-his2-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>  This accelerated Ireland\'s transition from a predominantly agricultural economy, into a <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_economy" title="Knowledge economy">knowledge economy</a> focused on attracting US multinationals from high-tech, life sciences and financial services industries, seeking to avail of Ireland\'s attractive corporate tax rates and unique <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">corporate tax system</a>.\n</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland#Multinational_tax_schemes" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">multinational tax schemes</a>" foreign firm use in Ireland materially distort Irish economic statistics.  This reached a climax with the famous "<a href="/wiki/Leprechaun_economics" title="Leprechaun economics">leprechaun economics</a>" GDP/GNP growth rates of 2015 (as Apple restructured its Irish subsidiaries in 2015).  The <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_Ireland" title="Central Bank of Ireland">Central Bank of Ireland</a> introduced a new statistic, "<a href="/wiki/Modified_gross_national_income" title="Modified gross national income">modified GNI</a>" (or GNI*), to remove these distortions. GNI* is 30% below GDP (or, GDP is 143% of GNI).<sup id="cite_ref-gni1_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gni1-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gni2_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gni2-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>  Ireland\'s GDP and GNP should no longer be used.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-esrg_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esrg-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the creation of the <a href="/wiki/International_Financial_Services_Centre" title="International Financial Services Centre">IFSC</a>, the country experienced strong and sustained economic growth, which fuelled a dramatic rise in Irish consumer borrowing and spending, and Irish construction and investment, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Tiger" title="Celtic Tiger">Celtic Tiger</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-aw_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aw-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>   By 2007, Ireland had the highest private sector debt in the OCED with a household debt-to-disposable income ratio of 190%.  Global capital markets, who had financed Ireland\'s build-up of debt in the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Tiger" title="Celtic Tiger">Celtic Tiger</a> period, by enabling Irish banks to borrow in excess of the domestic deposit base (to over 180% at peak<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>), withdrew support in the <a href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Global financial crisis">global financial crisis</a>.  Their withdrawal, from an over-borrowed Irish credit system, would precipitate a deep Irish property correction, which would then lead to the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9314_Irish_banking_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2008\xe2\x80\x9314 Irish banking crisis">Irish banking system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aw_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aw-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland\'s successful "low-tax" economy opens it to accusations of being a "corporate <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-txh1_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-txh1-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-txh2_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-txh2-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-txh3_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-txh3-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and led to it being "black listed".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>  A 2017 study ranks Ireland as the 5th largest global <a href="/wiki/Conduit_and_Sink_OFCs" title="Conduit and Sink OFCs">Conduit OFC</a> (conduits legally route funds to <a href="/wiki/Tax_havens" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax havens">tax havens</a>).  A serious challenge is the passing of the US <a href="/wiki/Tax_Cuts_and_Jobs_Act_of_2017" title="Tax Cuts and Jobs Act of 2017">Tax Cuts and Jobs Act of 2017</a> (whose FDII and GILTI regimes target Ireland\'s "<a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland#Multinational_tax_schemes" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">multinational tax schemes</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-home_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-home-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-trump_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trump-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbrx_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbrx-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>  The EU\'s 2018 Digital Sales Tax (DST)<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> (and desire for a <a href="/wiki/Common_Consolidated_Corporate_Tax_Base" title="Common Consolidated Corporate Tax Base">CCCTB</a><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>), is also seen as an attempt to restrict Irish "<a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland#Multinational_tax_schemes" title="Corporation tax in the Republic of Ireland">multinational tax schemes</a>" by US technology firms.<sup id="cite_ref-cofxx_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cofxx-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-euu2_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euu2-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-euu1_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euu1-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:La_Touche_House,_Dublin_(_DSC6350).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg/220px-La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg/330px-La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg/440px-La_Touche_House%2C_Dublin_%28_DSC6350%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3539" data-file-height="2369" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:La_Touche_House,_Dublin_(_DSC6350).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/International_Financial_Services_Centre" title="International Financial Services Centre">International Financial Services Centre</a> in Dublin</div></div></div>\n<p>Although multinational corporations dominate Ireland\'s export sector, exports from other sources also contribute significantly to the national income. The activities of multinational companies based in Ireland have made it one of the largest exporters of pharmaceutical agents, medical devices and software-related goods and services in the world. Ireland\'s exports also relate to the activities of large Irish companies (such as <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Group" title="Kerry Group">Kerry Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smurfit_Kappa" title="Smurfit Kappa">Smurfit Kappa</a>) and exports of mineral resources: Ireland is the seventh largest producer of zinc concentrates, and the twelfth largest producer of lead concentrates. The country also has significant deposits of <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, and smaller quantities of copper, silver, gold, <a href="/wiki/Barite" class="mw-redirect" title="Barite">barite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Tourism in Ireland">Tourism in Ireland</a> contributes about 4% of GDP and is a significant source of employment.\n</p><p>Other goods exports include agri-food, cattle, beef, dairy products, and aluminum. Ireland\'s major imports include data processing equipment, chemicals, petroleum and petroleum products, textiles, and clothing. <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">Financial services</a> provided by multinational corporations based at the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Financial_Services_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Financial Services Centre">Irish Financial Services Centre</a> also contribute to Irish exports. The difference between exports (\xe2\x82\xac89.4 billion) and imports (\xe2\x82\xac45.5 billion) resulted an annual trade surplus of \xe2\x82\xac43.9 billion in 2010, which is the highest trade surplus relative to GDP achieved by any EU member state.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The EU is by far the country\'s largest trading partner, accounting for 57.9% of exports and 60.7% of imports. The United Kingdom is the most important trading partner within the EU, accounting for 15.4% of exports and 32.1% of imports. Outside the EU, the United States accounted for 23.2% of exports and 14.1% of imports in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg/220px-IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg/330px-IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg/440px-IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2344" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IMG_WindfarmKilmuck1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wind farm in <a href="/wiki/County_Wexford" title="County Wexford">County Wexford</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Ireland" title="Energy in Ireland">Energy in Ireland</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/ESB_Electric_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="ESB Electric Ireland">ESB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bord_G%C3%A1is_Energy" title="Bord G\xc3\xa1is Energy">Bord G\xc3\xa1is Energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airtricity" title="Airtricity">Airtricity</a> are the three main electricity and gas suppliers in Ireland. There are 19.82 billion cubic metres of proven reserves of gas.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Natural gas extraction previously occurred at the <a href="/wiki/Kinsale_Head_gas_field" title="Kinsale Head gas field">Kinsale Head</a> until its exhaustion. The <a href="/wiki/Corrib_gas_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Corrib gas field">Corrib gas field</a> was due to come on stream in 2013/14. In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Barryroe" title="Barryroe">Barryroe</a> field was confirmed to have up to 1.6 billion barrels of oil in reserve, with between 160 and 600 million recoverable.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> That could provide for Ireland\'s entire energy needs for up to 13 years, when it is developed in 2015/16. There have been significant efforts to increase the use of renewable and sustainable forms of energy in Ireland, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Wind power in the Republic of Ireland">wind power</a>, with 3,000 MegaWatts<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a> being constructed, some for the purpose of export.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Energy_Authority_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable Energy Authority of Ireland">Sustainable Energy Authority of Ireland</a> (SEAI) has estimated that 6.5% of Ireland\'s 2011 energy requirements were produced by renewable sources.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The SEAI has also reported an increase in energy efficiency in Ireland with a 28% reduction in carbon emissions per house from 2005 to 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Ireland" title="Transport in Ireland">Transport in Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Ireland" title="Rail transport in Ireland">Rail transport in Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roads_in_Ireland" title="Roads in Ireland">Roads in Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerfort-bac.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Aerfort-bac.jpg/220px-Aerfort-bac.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Aerfort-bac.jpg/330px-Aerfort-bac.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Aerfort-bac.jpg/440px-Aerfort-bac.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2568" data-file-height="1428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerfort-bac.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terminal 1 and 2 at <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Airport" title="Dublin Airport">Dublin Airport</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The country\'s three main <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airports</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Airport" title="Dublin Airport">Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shannon_Airport" title="Shannon Airport">Shannon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cork_Airport" title="Cork Airport">Cork</a> serve many European and intercontinental routes with scheduled and <a href="/wiki/Air_charter" title="Air charter">chartered</a> flights. The London to Dublin air route is the ninth busiest international air route in the world, and also the busiest international air route in Europe, with 14,500 flights between the two in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-second-busiest_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-second-busiest-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, 4.5 million people took the route, at that time, the world\'s second-busiest.<sup id="cite_ref-second-busiest_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-second-busiest-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a> is the flag carrier of Ireland, although <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a> is the country\'s largest airline. Ryanair is Europe\'s largest low-cost carrier,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> the second largest in terms of passenger numbers, and the world\'s largest in terms of international passenger numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-watspax2007_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watspax2007-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Intercity_Heuston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Intercity_Heuston.jpg/220px-Intercity_Heuston.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Intercity_Heuston.jpg/330px-Intercity_Heuston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Intercity_Heuston.jpg/440px-Intercity_Heuston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Intercity_Heuston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/InterCity_(Iarnr%C3%B3d_%C3%89ireann)" title="InterCity (Iarnr\xc3\xb3d \xc3\x89ireann)">InterCity</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_4_(Iarnr%C3%B3d_%C3%89ireann)" title="Mark 4 (Iarnr\xc3\xb3d \xc3\x89ireann)">Mark IV</a> train at <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Heuston_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Dublin Heuston railway station">Heuston station</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Railway services are provided by <a href="/wiki/Iarnr%C3%B3d_%C3%89ireann" title="Iarnr\xc3\xb3d \xc3\x89ireann">Iarnr\xc3\xb3d \xc3\x89ireann</a> (Irish Rail), which operates all internal <a href="/wiki/InterCity_(Irish_Rail)" class="mw-redirect" title="InterCity (Irish Rail)">intercity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commuter" class="mw-redirect" title="Commuter">commuter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freight" class="mw-redirect" title="Freight">freight</a> railway services in the country. Dublin is the centre of the network with two main stations, <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Heuston_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Dublin Heuston railway station">Heuston station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Connolly_railway_station" title="Dublin Connolly railway station">Connolly station</a>, linking to the country\'s cities and main towns. The <a href="/wiki/Enterprise_(train)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enterprise (train)">Enterprise</a> service, which runs jointly with <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Railways" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland Railways">Northern Ireland Railways</a>, connects Dublin and <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>. The whole of Ireland\'s mainline network operates on track with a <a href="/wiki/Track_gauge_in_Ireland" title="Track gauge in Ireland">gauge of 5&#160;ft 3&#160;in (1,600&#160;mm)</a>, which is unique in Europe and has resulted in distinct rolling stock designs. Dublin has a steadily improving public transport network including the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Area_Rapid_Transit" title="Dublin Area Rapid Transit">DART</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luas" title="Luas">Luas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Bus" title="Dublin Bus">Dublin Bus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dublinbikes" title="Dublinbikes">dublinbikes</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Motorways_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Motorways in the Republic of Ireland">Motorways</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_primary_road" title="National primary road">national primary roads</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_secondary_road" title="National secondary road">national secondary roads</a> are managed by the <a href="/wiki/National_Roads_Authority" title="National Roads Authority">National Roads Authority</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Regional_road_(Ireland)" title="Regional road (Ireland)">regional roads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_Roads_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Roads in Ireland">local roads</a> are managed by the local authorities in each of their respective areas. The road network is primarily focused on the capital, but motorways have been extended to other cities as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Transport_21" title="Transport 21">Transport 21</a></i> capital investment programme, as a result motorways have been completed between Dublin and a number of other major Irish cities including Cork, Limerick, Waterford and Galway.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dublin has been the focus of major projects such as the <a href="/wiki/East-Link_(Dublin)" title="East-Link (Dublin)">East-Link</a> and <a href="/wiki/West-Link" title="West-Link">West-Link</a> toll-bridges, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Port_Tunnel" title="Dublin Port Tunnel">Dublin Port Tunnel</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lynch_Tunnel" title="Jack Lynch Tunnel">Jack Lynch Tunnel</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/River_Lee_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Lee (Ireland)">River Lee</a> in Cork, and the <a href="/wiki/Limerick_Tunnel" title="Limerick Tunnel">Limerick Tunnel</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/River_Shannon" title="River Shannon">River Shannon</a>, were two major projects outside Dublin. Several by-pass projects are underway in other urban areas.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Demographics of the Republic of Ireland">Demographics of the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Irish_population_analysis" title="Irish population analysis">Irish population analysis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png/220px-Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png/330px-Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png/440px-Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png 2x" data-file-width="617" data-file-height="475" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Population_of_Ireland_1951-2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population of Ireland since 1951.</div></div></div>\n<p>Genetic research suggests that the earliest settlers migrated from <a href="/wiki/Iberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> following the most recent <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">ice age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, migrants introduced a <a href="/wiki/Celt" class="mw-redirect" title="Celt">Celtic</a> language and culture. Migrants from the two latter eras still represent the genetic heritage of most <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> tradition expanded and became the dominant form over time. Irish people are a combination of Gaelic, <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a>, French, and British ancestry.\n</p><p>The population of Ireland stood at 4,588,252 in 2011, an increase of 8.2% since 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Republic_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Ireland had the highest birth rate in the European Union (16 births per 1,000 of population).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, 36.3% of births were to unmarried women.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Annual population growth rates exceeded 2% during the 2002-2006 intercensal period, which was attributed to high rates of <a href="/wiki/Natural_increase" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural increase">natural increase</a> and immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> This rate declined somewhat during the subsequent 2006-2011 intercensal period, with an average annual percentage change of 1.6%.\n</p><p>At the time of the 2016 census, the number of non-Irish nationals was recorded at 535,475. This represents a 2% decrease from the 2011 census figure of 544,357. The five largest sources of non-Irish nationals were Poland (122,515), the UK (103,113), Lithuania (36,552), Romania (29,186) and Latvia (19,933) respectively. Compared with 2011, the number of UK, Polish, Lithuanian and Latvian nationals fell. There were four new additions to the top ten largest non-Irish nationalities in 2016: Brazilian (13,640), Spanish (12,112), Italian (11,732), and French (11,661).<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland_by_population" title="List of urban areas in the Republic of Ireland by population">List of urban areas in the Republic of Ireland by population</a></div>\n<table class="infobox" style="text-align:center; width:97%; margin-right:10px; font-size:90%">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="8" style="background:#e9e9e9; padding:0.3em; areas</span></h3>\n<p>The following is a list of <a href="/wiki/Larger_urban_zone" title="Larger urban zone">functional urban areas</a> in Ireland and their population as of  2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Republic_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:left;">\n\n<tbody><tr style="font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\n<th align="center">Functional urban areas\n</th>\n<th align="center">Population<br /><small>2014</small>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a></b>\n</td>\n<td align="right">1,836,119\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a></b>\n</td>\n<td align="right">371,004\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a></b>\n</td>\n<td align="right">209,669\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Limerick" title="Limerick">Limerick</a></b>\n</td>\n<td align="right">165,800\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a></b>\n</td>\n<td align="right">111,525\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Ireland" title="Languages of Ireland">Languages of Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-English" title="Hiberno-English">Hiberno-English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mid_Ulster_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid Ulster English">Mid Ulster English</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/250px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" width="250" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/375px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/500px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="10560" data-file-height="14885" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The percentage who said they spoke Irish daily outside the education system in the 2011 census.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Irish Constitution describes Irish as the "national language", but English is the dominant language. In the 2006 census, 39% of the population regarded themselves as competent in Irish. Irish is spoken as a community language only in a small number of rural areas mostly in the west and south of the country, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht" title="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</a>. Except in Gaeltacht regions, road signs are usually bilingual.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Most public notices and print media are in English only. While the state is officially bilingual, citizens can often struggle to access state services in Irish and most government publications are not available in both languages, even though citizens have the right to deal with the state in Irish. Irish language media include the TV channel <a href="/wiki/TG4" title="TG4">TG4</a>, the radio station <a href="/wiki/RT%C3%89_Raidi%C3%B3_na_Gaeltachta" title="RT\xc3\x89 Raidi\xc3\xb3 na Gaeltachta">RT\xc3\x89 Raidi\xc3\xb3 na Gaeltachta</a> and online newspaper <a href="/wiki/Tuairisc.ie" title="Tuairisc.ie">Tuairisc.ie</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Defence_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Defence Forces">Irish Defence Forces</a>, all foot and arms drill commands are given in the Irish language.\n</p><p>As a result of immigration, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> is the most widely spoken language in Ireland after English, with Irish as the third most spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>  Several other Central European languages (namely Czech, Hungarian and Slovak), as well as <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic languages</a> (Lithuanian and Latvian) are also spoken on a day-to-day basis. Other languages spoken in Ireland include <a href="/wiki/Shelta" title="Shelta">Shelta</a>, spoken by Irish Travellers, and a dialect of <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Scots</a> is spoken by some <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Ulster Scots people</a> in Donegal.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Most secondary school students choose to learn one or two foreign languages. Languages available for the Junior Certificate and the Leaving Certificate include French, German, Italian and Spanish; Leaving Certificate students can also study Arabic, Japanese and Russian. Some secondary schools also offer <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>. The study of Irish is compulsory for Leaving Certificate students, but some may qualify for an exemption in some circumstances, such as learning difficulties or entering the country after age 11.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Healthcare in the Republic of Ireland">Healthcare in the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg/220px-R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg/330px-R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg/440px-R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R.C.S.I_Disease_Research_Centre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/RCSI" class="mw-redirect" title="RCSI">RCSI</a> Disease and Research Centre at <a href="/wiki/Beaumont_Hospital,_Dublin" title="Beaumont Hospital, Dublin">Beaumont Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> city.</div></div></div>\n<p>Healthcare in Ireland is provided by both public and private healthcare providers.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Health_(Ireland)" title="Minister for Health (Ireland)">Minister for Health</a> has responsibility for setting overall health service policy. Every resident of Ireland is entitled to receive health care through the public health care system, which is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Health_Service_Executive" title="Health Service Executive">Health Service Executive</a> and funded by general taxation. A person may be required to pay a subsidised fee for certain health care received; this depends on income, age, illness or disability. All maternity services are provided free of charge and children up to the age of 6 months. Emergency care is provided to patients who present to a hospital emergency department. However, visitors to emergency departments in non-emergency situations who are not referred by their <a href="/wiki/General_practitioner" title="General practitioner">GP</a> may incur a fee of \xe2\x82\xac100. In some circumstances this fee is not payable or may be waived.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Anyone holding a <a href="/wiki/European_Health_Insurance_Card" title="European Health Insurance Card">European Health Insurance Card</a> is entitled to free maintenance and treatment in public beds in Health Service Executive and voluntary hospitals. Outpatient services are also provided for free. However, the majority of patients on median incomes or above are required to pay subsidised hospital charges. Private health insurance is available to the population for those who want to avail of it.\n</p><p>The average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> in Ireland in 2012 is 81 years (<a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> average life expectancy in 2012 was 80 years), with 78.2 years for men and 83.6 years for women.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> It has the highest birth rate in the EU (16.8 births per 1,000 inhabitants, compared to an EU average of 10.7)<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> and a very low infant mortality rate (3.5 per 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Live_birth_(human)" title="Live birth (human)">live births</a>). The Irish healthcare system ranked 13th out of 34 European countries in 2012 according to the European Health Consumer Index produced by <a href="/wiki/Health_Consumer_Powerhouse" title="Health Consumer Powerhouse">Health Consumer Powerhouse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The same report ranked the Irish healthcare system as having the 8th best health outcomes but only the 21st most accessible system in Europe.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Education in the Republic of Ireland">Education in the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG/220px-University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG/330px-University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG/440px-University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3424" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:University-College-Cork-Panorama-2012.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/University_College_Cork" title="University College Cork">University College Cork</a> was founded in 1845 and is a <i>constituent university</i> of the <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_Ireland" title="National University of Ireland">National University of Ireland</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Ireland has three levels of education: primary, secondary and higher education. The education systems are largely under the direction of the Government via the <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Education_and_Skills" title="Minister for Education and Skills">Minister for Education and Skills</a>. Recognised primary and secondary schools must adhere to the curriculum established by the relevant authorities. Education is compulsory between the ages of six and fifteen years, and all children up to the age of eighteen must complete the first three years of secondary, including one sitting of the <a href="/wiki/Junior_Certificate" title="Junior Certificate">Junior Certificate</a> examination.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are approximately 3,300 primary schools in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-DES_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DES-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The vast majority (92%) are under the patronage of the Catholic Church. Schools run by religious organisations, but receiving public money and recognition, cannot discriminate against pupils based upon religion or lack thereof. A sanctioned system of preference does exist, where students of a particular religion may be accepted before those who do not share the ethos of the school, in a case where a school\'s quota has already been reached.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Long_Room_Interior,_Trinity_College_Dublin,_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg/220px-Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg/330px-Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg/440px-Long_Room_Interior%2C_Trinity_College_Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9000" data-file-height="6796" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Long_Room_Interior,_Trinity_College_Dublin,_Ireland_-_Diliff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Longroom at the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College_Library" title="Trinity College Library">Trinity College Library</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Leaving_Certificate" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Leaving Certificate">Leaving Certificate</a>, which is taken after two years of study, is the final examination in the secondary school system. Those intending to pursue higher education normally take this examination, with access to third-level courses generally depending on results obtained from the best six subjects taken, on a competitive basis.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Third-level education awards are conferred by at least 38 Higher Education Institutions - this includes the constituent or linked colleges of seven universities, plus other designated institutions of the Higher Education and Training Awards Council.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a>, coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, currently ranks Ireland as having the fourth highest reading score, ninth highest science score and thirteenth highest mathematics score, among OECD countries, in its 2012 assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Irish students aged 15 years had the second highest levels of reading literacy in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Ireland also has 0.747 of the World\'s top 500 Universities per capita, which ranks the country in 8th place in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Primary, secondary and higher (university/college) level education are all free in Ireland for all EU citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> There are charges to cover student services and examinations.\n</p><p>In addition, 37 percent of Ireland\'s population has a university or <a href="/wiki/College_degree" class="mw-redirect" title="College degree">college degree</a>, which is among the highest percentages in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Religion in the Republic of Ireland">Religion in the Republic of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="barbox tleft" style="overflow-x: auto;">\n<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">\n<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">\n<tbody><tr style="background:#ddd"><th style="text-align:center;" colspan="5">Religion in the Republic of Ireland <sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup></th></tr>\n<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">\n<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:left;">Religion</td>\n<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>\n<td style="width:100px; text-align:left;"></td>\n<td style="padding:0 4px; width:1em; text-align:right;">Percent</td>\n<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a></td>\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:Yellow; width:78.3%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">78.3%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Non-religious</a></td>\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:Green; width:10.1%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">10.1%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a></td>\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:Violet; width:4.2%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">4.2%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam in Ireland">Muslim</a></td>\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:Black; width:1.3%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">1.3%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Other</td>\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:Orange; width:6.1%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">6.1%</td>\n</tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Religious freedom is constitutionally provided for in Ireland. <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Ireland" title="Christianity in Ireland">Christianity</a> is the predominant religion, and while Ireland remains a predominantly Catholic country, the percentage of the population who identified as Catholic on the census has fallen sharply from 84.2 percent in the 2011 census to 78.3 percent in the most recent 2016 census. Other results from the 2016 census are&#160;: 4.2%  <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a>, 1.3% as Muslim, and 9.8% as having no religion.<sup id="cite_ref-pop2016v2_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop2016v2-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">Georgetown University</a> study, before 2000 the country had one of the highest rates of regular <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Mass">Mass</a> attendance in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\nWhile daily attendance was 13% in 2006, there was a reduction in weekly attendance from 81% in 1990 to 48% in 2006, although the decline was reported as stabilising.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, it was reported that weekly Mass attendance in Dublin was just 18%, with it being even lower among younger generations.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Procath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Procath.jpg/220px-Procath.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Procath.jpg/330px-Procath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Procath.jpg/440px-Procath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Procath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s_Pro-Cathedral" title="St Mary&#39;s Pro-Cathedral">St Mary\'s Pro-Cathedral</a> is the seat of the Roman Catholic Church in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:14_St._Patricks_Cathedral,_Dublin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg/220px-14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg/330px-14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg/440px-14_St._Patricks_Cathedral%2C_Dublin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:14_St._Patricks_Cathedral,_Dublin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral,_Dublin" title="St Patrick&#39;s Cathedral, Dublin">St Patrick\'s Cathedral, Dublin</a>, is the national Cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>, at 2.7% of the population, is the second largest Christian denomination. Membership declined throughout the twentieth century, but experienced an increase early in the 21st century, as have other small Christian denominations. Significant Protestant denominations are the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church_in_Ireland" title="Methodist Church in Ireland">Methodist Church</a>. Immigration has contributed to a growth in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Hinduism in the Republic of Ireland">Hindu</a> and Muslim populations. In percentage terms, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> were the fastest growing religions, with increases of 100% and 70% respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland\'s patron saints are <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brigid_of_Kildare" title="Brigid of Kildare">Saint Bridget</a> and <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Saint Columba</a>. Saint Patrick is the only one commonly recognised as the patron saint. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Day" title="Saint Patrick&#39;s Day">Saint Patrick\'s Day</a> is celebrated on 17 March in Ireland and abroad as the Irish national day, with parades and other celebrations.\n</p><p>As with other predominantly Catholic European states, Ireland underwent a period of legal secularisation in the late twentieth century. In 1972, the article of the Constitution naming specific religious groups was deleted by the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_of_the_Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Fifth Amendment of the Constitution of Ireland">Fifth Amendment</a> in a referendum. Article 44 remains in the Constitution: "The State acknowledges that the homage of public worship is due to Almighty God. It shall hold His Name in reverence, and shall respect and honour religion." The article also establishes freedom of religion, prohibits endowment of any religion, prohibits the state from religious discrimination, and requires the state to treat religious and non-religious schools in a non-prejudicial manner.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">Religious studies</a> was introduced as an optional Junior Certificate subject in 2001. Although many schools are run by religious organisations, a secularist trend is occurring among younger generations.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Ireland" title="Culture of Ireland">Culture of Ireland</a></div>\n<p>Ireland\'s culture was for centuries predominantly <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic</a>, and it remains one of the six principal <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a> invasion in the 12th century, and gradual British conquest and colonisation beginning in the 16th century, Ireland became influenced by English and Scottish culture. Subsequently, Irish culture, though distinct in many aspects, shares characteristics with the <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Europe">Catholic Europe</a>, and other Celtic regions.
